        </div><div class="casAbstract">Researchers must find a sustainable way of providing the power our modern lifestyles demand.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxUs7wptoM7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhnz5z40Q_Khw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXhs1Kntrc%253D&amp;md5=75c8cd94c450a1682aa6d1bd9bfe1c1f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.11.048"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Scrosati, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Garche, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Batteries: Status, Prospects and Future</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Power Sources</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">195</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">9</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2419</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2430</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.11.048</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D195%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D2419-2430%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26issue%3D9%26author%3DB.%2BScrosati%26author%3DJ.%2BGarche%26title%3DLithium%2BBatteries%253A%2BStatus%252C%2BProspects%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2009.11.048&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b06230&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2009.11.048&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium batteries: Status, prospects and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Scrosati, Bruno; Garche, Juergen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">195</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2419-2430</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium batteries are characterized by high specific energy, high efficiency and long life.  These unique properties have made lithium batteries the power sources of choice for the consumer electronics market with a prodn. of the order of billions of units per yr.  These batteries are also expected to find a prominent role as ideal electrochem. storage systems in renewable energy plants, as well as power systems for sustainable vehicles, such as hybrid and elec. vehicles.  However, scaling up the lithium battery technol. for these applications is still problematic since issues such as safety, costs, wide operational temp. and materials availability, are still to be resolved.  This review focuses first on the present status of lithium battery technol., then on its near future development and finally it examines important new directions aimed at achieving quantum jumps in energy and power content.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpF3KOlj2NuArVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghZnCu-JMUZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXivF2htQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=7273fa7f9f8dd9462c29aac1df0c7420</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seo, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">7</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D710-721%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26issue%3D7%26author%3DS.-W.%2BKim%26author%3DD.-H.%2BSeo%26author%3DX.%2BMa%26author%3DG.%2BCeder%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26title%3DElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPotential%2BAlternatives%2Bto%2BCurrent%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201200026&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b06230&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201200026&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghZnCu-JMUZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M. D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Johnson, C. S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Funct.                 10.1002/adfm.201200691</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D23%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D947-958%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26issue%3D8%26author%3DM.%2BD.%2BSlater%26author%3DD.%2BKim%26author%3DE.%2BLee%26author%3DC.%2BS.%2BJohnson%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201200691&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b06230&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201200691&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">947-958</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghZnCu-JMUZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5');                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of Nitrogen-Doped Electrospun Carbon Nanofibers as Anode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Chen; Lu, Yao; Ge, Yeqian; Zhu, Jiadeng; Jiang, Han; Li, Yongqiang; Hu, Yi; Zhang, Xiangwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy Technology (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1440-1449</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ETNEFN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2194-4296</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers (CNFs) were synthesized using a facile electrospinning technique with the addn. of urea as a nitrogen-doping agent.  The amt. of urea was selectively adjusted to control the degree and effectiveness of N-doping.  The morphol. of N-doped CNFs was investigated by SEM, transmission electron microscopy, and X-ray diffraction, whereas their electrochem. performance was studied using cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic charge-discharge expts.  The nitrogen content of N-doped CNFs increased significantly from 11.31 % to 19.06 % when the doping amt. of urea increased from 0 % to 30 %.  N-doping also played an important role in improving the electrochem. performance of the CNFs by introducing more defects in the carbon structure.  Results showed that N-doped CNFs with the highest nitrogen content (19.06 %) exhibited the largest reversible capacity of 354 mAh g-1 under a c.d. of 50 mA g-1; and when the c.d. was increased to 1 A g-1, a capacity of 193 mAh g-1 was still maintained.  It is, therefore, demonstrated that N-doped CNFs have great potential as suitable sodium-ion battery anode material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7Uy6w0GGAw7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghZnCu-JMUZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVWnu7zE&amp;md5=06ddf59e92a19a634d726fb7c8bbaa6b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501588"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ghimbeu, C.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design materials based on group IVA elements (Si, Ge, and Sn) for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xing-Long; Guo, Yu-Guo; Wan, Li-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - An Asian Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1948-1958</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CAAJBI</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) represent the state-of-the-art technol. in rechargeable energy-storage devices and they currently occupy the prime position in the marketplace for powering an increasingly diverse range of applications.  However, the fast development of these applications has led to increasing demands being placed on advanced LIBs in terms of higher energy/power densities and longer life cycles.  For LIBs to meet these requirements, researchers have focused on active electrode materials, owing to their crucial roles in the electrochem. performance of batteries.  For anode materials, compds. based on Group IVA (Si, Ge, and Sn) elements represent one of the directions in the development of high-capacity anodes.  Although these compds. have many significant advantages when used as anode materials for LIBs, there are still some crit. problems to be solved before they can meet the high requirements for practical applications.  In this Focus Review, we summarize a series of rational designs for Group IVA-based anode materials, in terms of their chem. compns. and structures, that could address these problems, i.e., huge vol. variations during cycling, unstable surfaces/interfaces, and invalidation of transport pathways for electrons upon cycling.  These designs should at least include one of the following structural benefits: 1) Contain a sufficient no. of voids to accommodate the vol. variations during cycling; 2) adopt a "plum-pudding"-like structure to limit the vol. variations during cycling; 3) facilitate an efficient and permanent transport pathway for electrons and lithium ions; or 4) show stable surfaces/interfaces to stabilize the in situ formed SEI layers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVGOG7nu-VjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixv0ScozN2TA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXntF2ht7k%253D&amp;md5=808c59ac27fb63eb191e7722b5b906d6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1149/1.2127495"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boukamp, B.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixv0ScozN2TA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00114"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mitlin, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc. Chem. Res.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">48</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">6</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1657</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1665</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00114</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D48%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D1657-1665%26journal%3DAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%26issue%3D6%26author%3DZ.%2BLi%26author%3DJ.%2BDing%26author%3DD.%2BMitlin%26title%3DTin%2Band%2BTin%2BCompounds%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%2BAnodes%253A%2BPhase%2BTransformations%2Band%2BPerformance%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.accounts.5b00114&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b06230&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.accounts.5b00114&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhi; Ding, Jia; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1657-1665</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium ion batteries (NIB, NAB, SIB) are attracting interest as a potentially lower cost alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIB), with readily available and geog. democratic reserves of the metal.  Tin is one of most promising SIB anode materials, which alloys with up to 3.75 Na, leading to a charge storage capacity of 847 mAh g-1.  In this Account, we outline the state-of-the-art understanding regarding the sodiation-induced phase transformations and the assocd. performance in a range of Sn-based systems, treating metallic Sn and its alloys, tin oxide (SnO2), tin sulfide (SnS2/SnS), and tin phosphide (Sn4P3).  We first detail what is known about the sodiation sequence in metallic Sn, highlighting the most recent insight into the reactions prior to the terminal equil. Na15Sn4 intermetallic.  We explain why researchers argue that the equil. (phase diagram) series of phase transitions does not occur in this system, and rather why sodiation/desodiation proceeds through a series of metastable cryst. and amorphous structures.  We also outline the recent modeling-based insight regarding how this phase transition profoundly influences the mech. properties of the alloy, progressively changing the bonding and the near neighbor arrangement from "Sn-like" to "Na-like" in the process.  We then go on to discuss the sodiation reactions in SnO2.  We argue that while a substantial amt. of exptl. work already exists where the focus is on synthesis and testing of tin oxide-based nanocomposites, the exact sodiation sequence is just beginning to be understood.  Unlike in Sn and Sn alloys, where capacities near the theor. are reached at least early during cycling, SnO2 never quite achieves anything close to the 1398 mAh g-1 that would be possible with a combination of fully reversible conversion and alloying reactions.  We highlight recent work demonstrating that contrary to general expectations, it is the Sn to Na15Sn4 alloying reaction that is incomplete and hence limits the capacity of the electrode.  We also describe how the oxide conversion reaction goes through an intermediate SnO phase, and how its reversibility in a half-cell is highly dependent on the terminal anodic voltage.  We then present what is known about sodiation of tin sulfide and of tin phosphide phases, including emerging microstructural evidence that may explain why both the sulfides and the phosphides are unable to achieve their highly promising theor. capacities under conventional electrode testing conditions.  Finally, we provide a broad comparison of the capacity (cycling and rate) performance for a range of Sn based anode materials, and show that there may be indeed an optimum microstructural architecture.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGraHWeuL6zqAbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixv0ScozN2TA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVakurzM&amp;md5=7305836f3a3bbf9d5e81e811cd8ab069</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201402511"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ha, K.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, S.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na-insertion/extraction properties of SnO thick-film electrodes prepared by gas-deposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shimizu, Masahiro; Usui, Hiroyuki; Sakaguchi, Hiroki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO thick-film electrodes for Na-ion battery anodes were prepd. for the first time by a gas-deposition method, and we investigated the influence of fluoroethylene carbonate as an electrolyte additive on their anode performances.  The SnO electrode showed high reversible capacities of 580 mA h g-1 and 260 mA h g-1 at the first cycle and at the 20th cycle, resp., in an additive-free electrolyte.  The cycle performance obtained was much higher than that of an Sn electrode.  These results indicate that Na2O formed in the first Na-insertion process played a role as a matrix to alleviate a stress generated by drastic volumetric changes during alloying/dealloying reactions of Sn with Na.  When we used fluorothylene carbonate as an additive, the SnO electrode achieved 250 mA h g-1 at the 50th cycle with a good capacity retention.  The excellent cycle performance originates from a facile Na-ion transfer through a surface layer formed between the electrode and the electrolyte because the surface layer derived from additive prevents from making itself thicker by suppressing decompn. of the electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqXK08khAB-h7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8vqgXJvBHog"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvV2gu77O&amp;md5=c314d07ccb364c7c83672d3aa7a40bdd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201303702"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Mesoporous SnO Microspheres as High Capacity Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. - Eur.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical Mesoporous SnO Microspheres as High Capacity Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Dawei; Xie, Xiuqiang; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3192-3197</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CEUJED</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mesoporous SnO microspheres were synthesized by a hydrothermal method using NaSO4 as the morphol. directing agent.  Field emission SEM (FESEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) analyses showed that SnO microspheres consist of nanosheets with a thickness of about 20 nm.  Each nanosheet contains a mesoporous structure with a pore size of approx. 5 nm.  When applied as anode materials in Na-ion batteries, SnO microspheres exhibited high reversible sodium storage capacity, good cyclability and a satisfactory high rate performance.  Through ex situ XRD anal., it was found that Na+ ions first insert themselves into SnO crystals, and then react with SnO to generate cryst. Sn, followed by Na-Sn alloying with the formation of cryst. NaSn2 phase.  During the charge process, there are two slopes corresponding to the de-alloying of Na-Sn compds. and oxidization of Sn, resp.  The high sodium storage capacity and good electrochem. performance could be ascribed to the unique hierarchical mesoporous architecture of SnO microspheres.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrXdrhnu8DXbLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8vqgXJvBHog"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXisFWiu78%253D&amp;md5=744dcb004cf9eba80fc40d876769c221</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.03.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Two-Dimensional SnO Anodes with a Tunable Number of Atomic Layers for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Fan; Zhu, Jiajie; Zhang, Daliang; Schwingenschlogl, Udo; Alshareef, Husam N.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have systematically changed the no. of at. layers stacked in 2D SnO nanosheet anodes and studied their sodium ion battery (SIB) performance.  The results indicate that as the no. of at. SnO layers in a sheet decreases, both the capacity and cycling stability of the Na ion battery improve.  The thinnest SnO nanosheet anodes (two to six SnO monolayers) exhibited the best performance.  Specifically, an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1072 and 848 mAh g-1 were obsd., resp., at 0.1 A g-1.  In addn., an impressive reversible capacity of 665 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 and 452 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles at a high c.d. of 1.0 A g-1 was obsd., with excellent rate performance.  As the av. no. of at. layers in the anode sheets increased, the battery performance degraded significantly.  For example, for the anode sheets with 10-20 at. layers, only a reversible capacity of 389 mAh g-1 could be obtained after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.  D. functional theory calcns. coupled with exptl. results were used to elucidate the sodiation mechanism of the SnO nanosheets.  This systematic study of monolayer-dependent phys. and electrochem. properties of 2D anodes shows a promising pathway to engineering and mitigating vol. changes in 2D anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  It also demonstrates that ultrathin SnO nanosheets are promising SIB anode materials with high specific capacity, stable cyclability, and excellent rate performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGok9B2_90thmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0Hz1BfhRN4A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1ags7s%253D&amp;md5=fe1ee09c0528efe249c2ef4f38511755</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.matlet.2007.08.022"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Majumdar, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chakraborty, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Devi, P.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced low-temperature lithium storage performance of multilayer graphene made through an improved ionic liquid-assisted synthesis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Raccichini, Rinaldo; Varzi, Alberto; Chakravadhanula, Venkata Sai Kiran; Kuebel, Christian; Balducci, Andrea; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of graphene are strongly depending on its synthesis.  Between the different methods proposed so far, liq. phase exfoliation turns out to be a promising method for the prodn. of graphene.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting the power performance of multilayer graphene as lithium-ion battery anode via unconventional doping with in-situ formed Fe nanoparticles</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Raccichini, Rinaldo; Varzi, Alberto; Chakravadhanula, Venkata Sai Kiran; Kuebel, Christian; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific Reports</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene is extensively investigated and promoted as a viable replacement for graphite, the state-of-the-art material for lithium-ion battery (LIB) anodes, although no clear evidence is available about improvements in terms of cycling stability, delithiation voltage and volumetric capacity.  Here we report the microwave-assisted synthesis of a novel graphene-based material in ionic liq. (i.e., carved multilayer graphene with nested Fe3O4 nanoparticles), together with its extensive characterization via several phys. and chem. techniques.  When such a composite material is used as LIB anode, the carved paths traced by the Fe3O4 nanoparticles, and the unconverted metallic iron formed in-situ upon the 1st lithiation, result in enhanced rate capability and, esp. at high specific currents (i.e., 5 A g-1), remarkable cycling stability (99% of specific capacity retention after 180 cycles), low av. delithiation voltage (0.244 V) and a substantially increased volumetric capacity with respect to com. graphite (58.8 Ah L-1 vs. 9.6 Ah L-1).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph7sgKG9zfN7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljljOXkJwD6kQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xlt1Gntbs%253D&amp;md5=8841e0b3056d555aa178cbfe5d50a794</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/am200973k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ito, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ohsawa, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorinated Ethylene Carbonate as Electrolyte Additive for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorinated Ethylene Carbonate as Electrolyte Additive for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Murata, Wataru; Ito, Atsushi; Ohsawa, Yasuhiko</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

                        cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Failure Mechanism of SnO2 Nanowires for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Meng; Kushima, Akihiro; Shao, Yuyan; Zhang, Ji-Guang; Liu, Jun; Browning, Nigel D.; Li, Ju; Wang, Chongmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5203-5211</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-lithium metals such as Na have attracted attention as a potential charge carrying ion for rechargeable batteries.  Using in situ TEM in combination with d. functional theory calcns., the authors probed the structural and chem. evolution of SnO2 nanowire anodes in Na-ion batteries and compared them quant. with results from Li-ion batteries.  Upon Na insertion into SnO2, a displacement reaction occurs, giving amorphous NaxSn nanoparticles dispersed in Na2O matrix.  With further Na insertion, the NaxSn crystd. into Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75).  Upon extn. of Na (de-sodiation), the NaxSn transforms to Sn nanoparticles.  Assocd. with the de-alloying, pores form, leading to a structure of Sn particles confined in a hollow matrix of Na2O.  These pores greatly increase elec. impedance, therefore accounting for the poor cyclability of SnO2.  DFT calcns. indicate that Na+ diffuses 30 times slower than Li+ in SnO2, in agreement with in situ TEM measurement.  Insertion of Na can chemomechanically soften the reaction product to a greater extent than in lithiation.  Therefore, in contrast to the lithiation of SnO2 significantly less dislocation plasticity was seen ahead of the sodiation front.  This direct comparison of the results from Na and Li highlights the crit. role of ionic size and electronic structure of different ionic species on the charge/discharge rate and failure mechanisms in these batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxbnVD4FT9W7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljljOXkJwD6kQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFels7zP&amp;md5=3eeb24d9885b1c825918e689b7adbbb2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1007/s0021663550654"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Süzer, Ş.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Voscoboinikov, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hallam, K.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The In and Sn 3d3/2 and 3d5/2 ESCA peaks and the O 1s peak of Sn-doped In2O3 films were compared with those for In2O3 films and In2O3, SnO, SnO2, and Sn3O4 powders.  Comparison of as-grown with sanded surfaces revealed Sn-rich surface layers in those films having good optical and transport properties.  These exptl. findings are interpreted with a schematic energy-band model and the assumption that film darkening in Sn-doped In2O3 films is caused by the formation and growth of an Sn3O4-like 2nd phase in the bulk.  Suppression of this phase could be accomplished by higher substrate temps., which permit equil. conditions to be attained: Sn-rich phases appear to migrate to the film surface, and the Sn disproportionates to Sn2+ and Sn4+ ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqLyxOkGptLrbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljljOXkJwD6kQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaE2sXlt1Gjurg%253D&amp;md5=1298b178b352c3952c484dc4f702b33c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/am5083683"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Duan, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qin, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rigid–Flexible Coupling High Ionic Conductivity Polymer Electrolyte for an Enhanced Performance of LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Graphite Battery at Elevated Temperature</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">8</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4720</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4727</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/am5083683</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D4720-4727%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26issue%3D8%26author%3DP.%2BHu%26author%3DY.%2BDuan%26author%3DD.%2BHu%26author%3DB.%2BQin%26author%3DJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BLiu%26author%3DG.%2BCui%26author%3DL.%2BChen%26title%3DRigid%25E2%2580%2593Flexible%2BCoupling%2BHigh%2BIonic%2BConductivity%2BPolymer%2BElectrolyte%2Bfor%2Ban%2BEnhanced%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BLiMn2O4%252FGraphite%2BBattery%2Bat%2BElevated%2BTemperature%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fam5083683&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b06230&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fam5083683&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rigid-flexible coupling high ionic cond. polymer electrolyte for an enhanced performance of LiMn2O4/graphite battery at elevated temp.</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Pu; Duan, Yulong; Hu, Deping; Qin, Bingsheng; Zhang, Jianjun; Wang, Qingfu; Liu, Zhihong; Cui, Guanglei; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4720-4727</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiMn2O4-based batteries exhibit severe capacity fading during cycling or storage in LiPF6-based liq. electrolytes, esp. at elevated temps.  Herein, a novel rigid-flexible gel polymer electrolyte is introduced to enhance the cyclability of LiMn2O4/graphite battery at elevated temp.  The polymer electrolyte consists of a robust natural cellulose skeletal incorporated with soft segment poly(Et α-cyanoacrylate).  The introduction of the cellulose effectively overcomes the drawback of poor mech. integrity of the gel polymer electrolyte.  D. functional theory (DFT) calcn. demonstrates that the poly(Et α-cyanoacrylate) matrixes effectively dissoc. the lithium salt to facilitate ionic transport and thus has a higher ionic cond. at room temp.  Ionic cond. of the gel polymer electrolyte is 3.3 × 10-3 S cm-1 at room temp.  The gel polymer electrolyte remarkably improves the cycling performance of LiMn2O4-based batteries, esp. at elevated temps.  The capacity retention after the 100th cycle is 82% at 55 °C, which is much higher than that of liq. electrolyte (1 M LiPF6 in carbonate solvents).  The polymer electrolyte can significantly suppress the dissoln. of Mn2+ from surface of LiMn2O4 because of strong interaction energy of Mn2+ with PECA, which was investigated by DFT calcn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph3smVX9rNjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljljOXkJwD6kQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXit12rs7c%253D&amp;md5=71aa6677f9efdb2b54193f322b96b14f</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8d981bf2b325-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium batteries are characterized by high specific energy, high efficiency and long life.  These unique properties have made lithium batteries the power sources of choice for the consumer electronics market with a prodn. of the order of billions of units per yr.  These batteries are also expected to find a prominent role as ideal electrochem. storage systems in renewable energy plants, as well as power systems for sustainable vehicles, such as hybrid and elec. vehicles.  However, scaling up the lithium battery technol. for these applications is still problematic since issues such as safety, costs, wide operational temp. and materials availability, are still to be resolved.  This review focuses first on the present status of lithium battery technol., then on its near future development and finally it examines important new directions aimed at achieving quantum jumps in energy and power content.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpF3KOlj2NuArVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh_QU9nNAix3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXivF2htQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=7273fa7f9f8dd9462c29aac1df0c7420</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seo, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh_QU9nNAix3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh_QU9nNAix3A"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of Nitrogen-Doped Electrospun Carbon Nanofibers as Anode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Chen; Lu, Yao; Ge, Yeqian; Zhu, Jiadeng; Jiang, Han; Li, Yongqiang; Hu, Yi; Zhang, Xiangwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy Technology (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers (CNFs) were synthesized using a facile electrospinning technique with the addn. of urea as a nitrogen-doping agent.  The amt. of urea was selectively adjusted to control the degree and effectiveness of N-doping.  The morphol. of N-doped CNFs was investigated by SEM, transmission electron microscopy, and X-ray diffraction, whereas their electrochem. performance was studied using cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic charge-discharge expts.  The nitrogen content of N-doped CNFs increased significantly from 11.31 % to 19.06 % when the doping amt. of urea increased from 0 % to 30 %.  N-doping also played an important role in improving the electrochem. performance of the CNFs by introducing more defects in the carbon structure.  Results showed that N-doped CNFs with the highest nitrogen content (19.06 %) exhibited the largest reversible capacity of 354 mAh g-1 under a c.d. of 50 mA g-1; and when the c.d. was increased to 1 A g-1, a capacity of 193 mAh g-1 was still maintained.  It is, therefore, demonstrated that N-doped CNFs have great potential as suitable sodium-ion battery anode material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7Uy6w0GGAw7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liuB0t-Niyynw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVWnu7zE&amp;md5=06ddf59e92a19a634d726fb7c8bbaa6b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501588"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ghimbeu, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) represent the state-of-the-art technol. in rechargeable energy-storage devices and they currently occupy the prime position in the marketplace for powering an increasingly diverse range of applications.  However, the fast development of these applications has led to increasing demands being placed on advanced LIBs in terms of higher energy/power densities and longer life cycles.  For LIBs to meet these requirements, researchers have focused on active electrode materials, owing to their crucial roles in the electrochem. performance of batteries.  For anode materials, compds. based on Group IVA (Si, Ge, and Sn) elements represent one of the directions in the development of high-capacity anodes.  Although these compds. have many significant advantages when used as anode materials for LIBs, there are still some crit. problems to be solved before they can meet the high requirements for practical applications.  In this Focus Review, we summarize a series of rational designs for Group IVA-based anode materials, in terms of their chem. compns. and structures, that could address these problems, i.e., huge vol. variations during cycling, unstable surfaces/interfaces, and invalidation of transport pathways for electrons upon cycling.  These designs should at least include one of the following structural benefits: 1) Contain a sufficient no. of voids to accommodate the vol. variations during cycling; 2) adopt a "plum-pudding"-like structure to limit the vol. variations during cycling; 3) facilitate an efficient and permanent transport pathway for electrons and lithium ions; or 4) show stable surfaces/interfaces to stabilize the in situ formed SEI layers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVGOG7nu-VjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liuB0t-Niyynw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXntF2ht7k%253D&amp;md5=808c59ac27fb63eb191e7722b5b906d6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1149/1.2127495"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boukamp, B.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSPCGmY-6BZg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00114"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mitlin, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc.                         rightTab-cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhi; Ding, Jia; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1657-1665</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium ion batteries (NIB, NAB, SIB) are attracting interest as a potentially lower cost alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIB), with readily available and geog. democratic reserves of the metal.  Tin is one of most promising SIB anode materials, which alloys with up to 3.75 Na, leading to a charge storage capacity of 847 mAh g-1.  In this Account, we outline the state-of-the-art understanding regarding the sodiation-induced phase transformations and the assocd. performance in a range of Sn-based systems, treating metallic Sn and its alloys, tin oxide (SnO2), tin sulfide (SnS2/SnS), and tin phosphide (Sn4P3).  We first detail what is known about the sodiation sequence in metallic Sn, highlighting the most recent insight into the reactions prior to the terminal equil. Na15Sn4 intermetallic.  We explain why researchers argue that the equil. (phase diagram) series of phase transitions does not occur in this system, and rather why sodiation/desodiation proceeds through a series of metastable cryst. and amorphous structures.  We also outline the recent modeling-based insight regarding how this phase transition profoundly influences the mech. properties of the alloy, progressively changing the bonding and the near neighbor arrangement from "Sn-like" to "Na-like" in the process.  We then go on to discuss the sodiation reactions in SnO2.  We argue that while a substantial amt. of exptl. work already exists where the focus is on synthesis and testing of tin oxide-based nanocomposites, the exact sodiation sequence is just beginning to be understood.  Unlike in Sn and Sn alloys, where capacities near the theor. are reached at least early during cycling, SnO2 never quite achieves anything close to the 1398 mAh g-1 that would be possible with a combination of fully reversible conversion and alloying reactions.  We highlight recent work demonstrating that contrary to general expectations, it is the Sn to Na15Sn4 alloying reaction that is incomplete and hence limits the capacity of the electrode.  We also describe how the oxide conversion reaction goes through an intermediate SnO phase, and how its reversibility in a half-cell is highly dependent on the terminal anodic voltage.  We then present what is known about sodiation of tin sulfide and of tin phosphide phases, including emerging microstructural evidence that may explain why both the sulfides and the phosphides are unable to achieve their highly promising theor. capacities under conventional electrode testing conditions.  Finally, we provide a broad comparison of the capacity (cycling and rate) performance for a range of Sn based anode materials, and show that there may be indeed an optimum microstructural architecture.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGraHWeuL6zqAbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSPCGmY-6BZg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVakurzM&amp;md5=7305836f3a3bbf9d5e81e811cd8ab069</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201402511"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ha, K.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This review provides a comprehensive overview of selected anode materials with high reversible capacities that can increase the energy d. of Na-ion batteries.  The reaction and failure mechanisms of these anode materials with a view to suggesting promising strategies for improving their electrochem. performance are also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrtUvBrd2blj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSPCGmY-6BZg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtleisb3E&amp;md5=cc353e03e6d1882496750bc1f80a382a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b04178"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kalubarme, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Constructing heterostructures can endow materials with fascinating performance in high-speed electronics, optoelectronics, and other applications owing to the built-in charge-transfer driving force, which is of benefit to the specific charge-transfer kinetics.  Rational design and controllable synthesis of nano-heterostructure anode materials with high-rate performance, however, still remains a great challenge.  Herein, ultrafine SnS/SnO2 heterostructures were successfully fabricated and showed enhanced charge-transfer capability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO thick-film electrodes for Na-ion battery anodes were prepd. for the first time by a gas-deposition method, and we investigated the influence of fluoroethylene carbonate as an electrolyte additive on their anode performances.  The SnO electrode showed high reversible capacities of 580 mA h g-1 and 260 mA h g-1 at the first cycle and at the 20th cycle, resp., in an additive-free electrolyte.  The cycle performance obtained was much higher than that of an Sn electrode.  These results indicate that Na2O formed in the first Na-insertion process played a role as a matrix to alleviate a stress generated by drastic volumetric changes during alloying/dealloying reactions of Sn with Na.  When we used fluorothylene carbonate as an additive, the SnO electrode achieved 250 mA h g-1 at the 50th cycle with a good capacity retention.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We have systematically changed the no. of at. layers stacked in 2D SnO nanosheet anodes and studied their sodium ion battery (SIB) performance.  The results indicate that as the no. of at. SnO layers in a sheet decreases, both the capacity and cycling stability of the Na ion battery improve.  The thinnest SnO nanosheet anodes (two to six SnO monolayers) exhibited the best performance.  Specifically, an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1072 and 848 mAh g-1 were obsd., resp., at 0.1 A g-1. For example, for the anode sheets with 10-20 at. layers, only a reversible capacity of 389 mAh g-1 could be obtained after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.
